O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, through its completion and for its explanations and declarations, it seems to me that I should complete that part of the operation with your hands, which is surgery.
Because the operation with hands is neglected in our region and in our time, to such an extent that it endangers the science of medicine itself, and its vestiges are suppressed; there remain only a few brief descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have been distorted and lead those who follow them into error and hesitation, while their minds are closed off and the strength and art of surgery is stretched out.
It seems to me that I should revive it by the arrangement of this treatise in accordance with the method of exposition and explanation and abridgment; and also for the forms of cautery irons and other instruments of operation as well as for their use, through the addition of explanation, and preparation, which is necessary.
The reason why a good artisan is not found with his hand in these our times is because the art of medicine is long, and its practitioner must first be exercised in the science of anatomy which considers the joints of the limbs and their forms, and the connection and disconnection of those; and have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and sinews, as well as their number and course; and of the pulsing and quiet veins, and the places for their exit.
And Hippocrates says that it has a great name, but little effect in practice, and especially in the art of medicine.
We have already mentioned this in the introduction of this book; for one who is ignorant of what we have said about anatomy cannot be excused if he falls into error and thereby causes the death of a man, as I myself have seen happen to many such individuals, because some are educated in this science but neglect their knowledge and practice.
I saw a foolish and inexperienced doctor make an incision into a scrofulous abscess on the neck of a woman, and he cut some arteries in the neck, causing so much blood to flow that the woman died between her sheets.
I saw another doctor who was about to remove a stone from a man's bladder, which had caused him great pain; and he brought it out by cutting open the bladder, but this led to the man's death three days later.
I have already been called upon to remove such a stone and saw that due to the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew he would die.

Regarding the cauterization of toothache.

Which contains the full discussion on abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses come in various forms depending on their cause, which we will explore further within this book.
Their treatment varies depending on the type of abscess and its cause, one method involves direct incision to treat the abscess itself while the other addresses underlying moisture-related issues.
And the second method is to treat wounds that occur in different parts of the body, because an abscess on the head requires different treatment than one on the buttocks; and an abscess on a fleshy part calls for another approach compared to one on a joint; each of them has its own specific surgical procedure.
Some abscesses should not be pierced but rather left to mature, or until they have completely healed on their own.
Other abscesses should be lanced when they are severe and not yet ripe for drainage; in particular, those located near joints, because if an abscess is next to a joint, its presence prolongs the healing process until you can remove what's inside it, and often the ligaments or nerves of that joint become corrupted, causing the limb to contract. Or consider one situated on a major organ: delaying its lancing after it has ripened will damage the main body part; or when located near the anus, pierce even unripe ones in order to prevent the pus from spreading inside the anal canal and becoming elongated toward the deeper parts of the rectum—forming a fistula or preventing healing within that area. It is not advisable to lance it while it remains hard due to incomplete digestion, but only when close to the anus, so you don't risk damaging its sphincter muscles nor lengthen any eventual cure. On venesection (bloodletting) from the veins:
The veins that are commonly used for bloodletting, as mentioned in chapter xvi, include two pulse points located near the ears (called alhasisain); two herbs appearing on each temple; two veins at the inner corners of the eyes, which are known as 'aspicientz'; a vein positioned in the middle of the forehead; and a vein found at the tip of the nose. Additionally, there are two jugulars situated by the neck; two veins on the upper lip; two veins on the lower lip; these latter veins are called "angelic"; and finally, there are two veins beneath the tongue. The veins in the arms that are typically used for bloodletting are five in number: one is the cephalic vein, which some call the 'wild latz'; people often refer to it as the 'head vein', and is a silent/hidden vein; another is the median cubital vein, composed of a branch from the basilic vein and another from the cephalic; locals name this the 'vein of the whole body'. The basilic vein lies in the domestic latz and is known as the 'tickling' or 'itching' vein by some, while others call it the 'belly vein'; there's also the brachial vein that sits above the elbow (where bloodletting occurs) and becomes visibly apparent when it appears on the biceps; the salvage vein lies between the auricular digit (little finger) and its smaller counterpart, containing two branches.

On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder."
The kneecap can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated to the outer side, the inner side, or below and behind; but not forward toward the front part of the leg.
The sign of a dislocation is when you ask the patient to apply their leg on their thigh, and if they cannot do so, then you will know that the kneecap is dislocated.
To restore the kneecap in all its dislocations, you must place the patient straight and ask them to extend their leg as far as they can; put your two hands on his face, and place his foot between your feet; then hold his knee with your palms, holding it firmly between your fingers over his knee cap while another assistant extends his foot until the kneecap returns to its original position.
The sign of the kneecap returning to its place is when their leg naturally sticks to his thigh without effort.

On the cure of ankle dislocation.
Sometimes the ankle is slightly bruised by a small blow, and sometimes it is dislocated depending on the direction of the force, with the dislocation occurring either towards the inner side or outwards.
The sign of ankle dislocation is that you will see the ankle swollen proceeding towards the side to which it has been dislocated.
But the cure for its bruise is a slight reduction, and it should be extended with ease using your hands and wrapped until it returns to normal.
But in the cure when it is dislocated according to the direction of force, you must make the patient lie down straight, and have someone strong and brave hold that side behind him at his middle.
And then you have your right hand on the upper part of their foot, and your left hand on the lower part of their leg near the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then use your left hand twice, and extend it again; use your right hand to move the foot away from the leg without causing strain.
Do this twice, as we have counted.
Then move the foot towards the leg for the third time, this time you are at the ankle.
So if you return the foot in one or two moves according to this method, and you see that the foot is aligned, then it's good; but if not, repeat the operation until the foot returns to its proper position.

Regarding the different types of dislocations caused by bruising, fracture or any other injury.
When you encounter any of these cases and engage in their treatment and restoration, it is evident that death often occurs multiple times.
